I 
 
FOREWORD 
Food composiƟon data provide evidence to inform policies, programs, and intervenƟons to support 
healthy and sustainable diets and food systems at the naƟonal, sub-regional, and conƟnental levels. They 
are a useful tool for various purposes, such as nutrient intake esƟmaƟon at individual and populaƟon 
levels,  dieteƟcs  and  clinical  pracƟces  to  formulate  diets,  food-based  dietary  guidelines  (FBDGs) 
development, industrial food product development, food regulaƟon and consumer protecƟon, nutriƟon 
labeling and forƟficaƟon, agricultural research, and food security and nutriƟon policy and planning. 
Despite their significant benefits for transforming food systems, country-specific Food ComposiƟon 
Tables (FCTs)–which provide data on the nutrient and energy content, as well as non-nutrients like 
phytochemicals, bioacƟve compounds, and anƟ-nutrients or toxic components in food–are scarce or 
outdated in many low- and middle-income countries (LMICs), including Ethiopia. Ethiopia developed its 
first FCT in 1969, and it has been over two decades since it was last updated.   
The current update is therefore necessary due to several reasons, including the introducƟon of new food 
items (such as processed, imported, and underuƟlized foods), changes in agricultural pracƟces which is 
likely to change the food composiƟon, changes in dietary paƩerns over the decades, advancements in 
analyƟcal methods, and the absence of nutrient values for certain foods in the previous versions of 
Ethiopian FCTs (EFCT) and advancement in nutriƟonal knowledge to explore addiƟonal components. 
With funding from the Gates FoundaƟon, the Ethiopian Public Health InsƟtute (EPHI), the technical arm 
of the Ministry of Health (MoH), and the Food and Agriculture OrganizaƟon of the United NaƟons (FAO) 
updated the EFCT with new analyƟcal data, on tradiƟonal/indigenous, and modern foods/recipes. 
The EPHI and FAO are therefore confident that this up-to-date food composiƟon data can be used by 
mulƟple sectors in the country, such as food processing industries for proper labeling of food items, 
dieƟcians and clinical pracƟƟoners to advise their paƟents’ diets, the agricultural sector to promote 
nutriƟonally important plant- and animal-based foods, the educaƟon sector to idenƟfy appropriate foods 
for the school meal program, and the food standard development authoriƟes to establish naƟonal and 
regional standards, and other relevant authoriƟes to monitor the effecƟveness of nutriƟon-related 
policies and programs, in addiƟon to research and academia.  
The EFCT 2025 was developed through a collaboraƟve effort involving various insƟtuƟons, ministries, 
organizaƟons, development partners, private sectors, laboratories, and individuals. We are grateful to all 
partners for their consistent support and contribuƟons in developing the EFCT 2025.  
 
 
Mesay Hailu (PhD)               Ms. Farayi Zimudzi  
Director General             FAO RepresentaƟve in Ethiopia 
Ethiopian Public Health InsƟtute         Food and Agriculture OrganizaƟon of the United NaƟon

1 
 
1. IntroducƟon  
Unhealthy diets around the world have become one of the leading risk factors for morbidity and 
mortality in populaƟons. For example, about 11 million deaths and 255 million disability-
adjusted life-years were aƩributed to dietary risk factors in 2017 only (Afshin et al., 2019). This 
emphasizes the importance of understanding people’s dietary paƩerns and the nutrient content 
of their food choices. The lack of individual-level dietary intake data coupled with lack of quality 
food composiƟon data, parƟcularly in low- and middle-income countries (LMICs), poses a major 
challenge in idenƟfying key dietary risk factors. Despite many metrics and indicators being 
tested and validated to understand dietary diversity and quality, data on the composiƟon of 
foods is sƟll limited or outdated.  
Food ComposiƟon Tables (FCTs) and Food ComposiƟon Databases (FCDBs) provide data on the 
nutrient and energy content of foods, and someƟmes include informaƟon on phytochemicals, 
bioacƟve compounds, anƟ-nutrients, and toxic components (Greenfield & Southgate, 2003). 
Food composiƟon data provide evidence to inform intervenƟons, policies, and programs to 
support healthy and sustainable diets and food systems at the naƟonal, sub-regional, and 
conƟnental levels. They are used as a tool for various purposes, such as nutrient intake 
esƟmaƟon at individual and populaƟon levels, dieteƟcs and clinical pracƟces to formulate diets, 
food-based dietary guidelines (FBDGs) development, food standard development, regulaƟon 
and  consumer  protecƟon,  nutriƟon  labeling  and  forƟficaƟon,  agricultural  research,  and 
nutriƟon and agriculture planning and policy (Charrondiere et al., 2011; Greenfield & Southgate, 
2003). Despite their mulƟple benefits to achieve the food system transformaƟon, country 
specific FCTs/FCDBs are scarce or outdated in LMICs, including Ethiopia.    
1.1 History of the Ethiopian FCT development: 1968–2025  
Ethiopia has published its first FCT in 1968 through a collaboraƟve effort of the Ethiopian 
NutriƟon InsƟtute (ENI) and the Ethio-Swedish Technical Assistance Project. The FCT included 
relevant data of about 180 Ethiopian foods, and it was a pioneer iniƟaƟve in the conƟnent 
(Agren & Gibson, 1968). The second Ethiopian FCT was developed by generaƟng data on amino 
acid and biological value of protein for the same food samples considered in the first FCT (Agren 
et  al.  1975).  The  project  was  accomplished  with  the  financial  support  of  the  Swedish 
InternaƟonal  Development  Authority  (SIDA)  and  the  Ethiopian  Ministry  of  Public  Health. 
However, both FCTs lacked some important data for processed and cooked foods (Agren & 
Gibson, 1968; Agren et al., 1975).  
To provide updated data, including data on processed and cooked foods, the third Ethiopian FCT 
was developed and published in 1997. The proximate composiƟon, energy, minerals (calcium, 

2 
 
iron and phosphorus), vitamins (beta carotene equivalent, thiamin, riboflavin, niacin, ascorbic 
acid),  tryptophan  ,  and  refuse factor of  about  700  food  items,  collected  from naƟonally 
representaƟve sampling sites, were analyzed (ENI & EHNRI, 1997) with the financial support 
from the government of Ethiopia through the Ethiopian Health and NutriƟon Research InsƟtute 
(EHNRI). However, none of the three FCTs covered foods from all the regions of Ethiopia, 
parƟcularly the peripheral areas of the country where foods and feeding habits differs from the 
central Ethiopia. Therefore, part IV of the Ethiopian FCT was developed to address the diverse 
and unique ethnic-based foods in the country (FAO & EHNRI, 1998). In this version, cooked 
(composite dishes) and raw foods commonly consumed in the specific locality were collected. 
Detailed informaƟon on preparaƟon methods and the ingredients used in every dish were also 
collected  and nineteen  nutriƟonal  parameters,  proximate  composiƟon,  energy,  minerals 
(calcium, phosphorus, potassium, iron, zinc, copper, sodium and potassium), vitamins (reƟnol 
equivalent, thiamin, riboflavin and ascorbic acid), were analyzed. The EHNRI and the Food and 
Agriculture OrganizaƟon of the United NaƟons (FAO) jointly supported the development of the 
fourth version of the FCT (FAO & EHNRI, 1998).  
It has been more than two decades since part IV of the Ethiopian FCT (EFCT) was published. The 
current update is necessary due to several reasons, including the introducƟon of new food 
items (such as processed, imported, and underuƟlized foods), changes in dietary paƩerns over 
the decades, advancements in analyƟcal methods, and the absence of nutrient values for 
certain foods and new nutrients (e.g., dietary fiber, folate, selenium) in the old FCTs. With 
funding from the Bill & Melinda Gates FoundaƟon (BMGF), the Ethiopian Public Health InsƟtute 
(EPHI) with technical support from FAO updated the EFCT with new data on raw foods, as well 
as tradiƟonal, indigenous, and modern foods and recipes. The updated EFCT (EFCT 2025) was 
prepared in compliance with guidelines and standards published by FAO and the InternaƟonal 
Network of Food Data Systems (INFOODS), a worldwide network of food composiƟon experts 
aiming to improve the quality, availability, reliability, and use of food composiƟon data. The 
EFCT 2025 provides food composiƟon data that can be used to esƟmate nutrient intakes, beƩer 
understand diet composiƟon in the country, promote food-based intervenƟons, strengthen food 
and nutriƟon research, advise food security and nutriƟon policies and programs, and support 
industrial food product development. 
1.2 ObjecƟves  
The aim of updaƟng the EFCT was to incorporate data for commonly consumed foods, as well as 
tradiƟonal,  indigenous, and  modern  foods  and  recipes. This  was  achieved  through  direct 
analysis of prioriƟzed food items, compilaƟon of addiƟonal analyƟcal data from various sources, 
and recipe calculaƟon for cooked single ingredient and mixed recipes. The specific objecƟves 
were: 

3 
 
1. To idenƟfy and fill the data gaps in terms of missing foods and nutrients to improve the 
coverage of the FCT. 
2. To provide reliable data of macro- and micro-nutrients of raw and cooked foods and 
recipes consumed in Ethiopia. 
3. To present and document the food composiƟon data in a user-friendly and easily 
accessible manner according to internaƟonal standards and guidelines. 
1.3 Outputs and structure  
The EFCT 2025 is structured and disseminated into three parts:  
(1) the user guide and condensed FCT which provides details on the components and foods 
included, methodologies used to generate, compile, calculate and present the data as well as 
data on 39 out of the 69 components for all foods listed in the EFCT 2025,   
(2) the full set of datasheets (in MicrosoŌ Excel) including 69 components for a total of 727 food 
entries listed in the EFCT 2025 with addiƟonal staƟsƟcs and documentaƟon at value level, and 
(3) a recipe book which was developed by collecƟng detailed informaƟon on recipe ingredients, 
their proporƟons, preparaƟon methods, and the final weight or volume of each recipe. Nutrient 
informaƟon for 145 recipes is documented. 
1.4 Stages of EFCT 2025 Development  
The process to update the EFCT comprised three main steps. First, the exisƟng naƟonal FCTs 
(parts I to IV) were compiled and evaluated. In parallel, exisƟng analyƟcal data for Ethiopian 
foods were compiled from various sources, such as published scienƟfic arƟcles, unpublished 
MSc thesis and PhD dissertaƟon, laboratory reports and conference proceedings and reviewed 
as per the INFOODS guideline. For prioriƟzed foods, a naƟonal sampling was employed, and 
nutrient analysis was conducted. Finally, detailed informaƟon was collected to standardize 
selected recipes and enable the calculaƟon of their nutrient profiles.  
1.5 Foods included in the EFCT 2025 
Previous versions of the EFCT together with the food lists from naƟonal surveys were the basis 
to idenƟfy foods or recipes to be included in the EFCT 2025, including tradiƟonal or indigenous 
and modern recipes. Accordingly, all the 1438 foods/recipes reported in the previous EFCTs 
(parts I to IV) were included. In a second step, commonly consumed foods and foods that were 
missing from the old EFCTs were idenƟfied through extensive search of naƟonal surveys. These 
sources include the 2011 Ethiopian NaƟonal Food ConsumpƟon Survey (NFCS, 2013), two 
Ethiopian Household Income, ConsumpƟon and Expenditure Surveys (HICE 2011; HICE 2016), 

4 
 
other published and unpublished documents and archives from relevant insƟtutes in the 
country, including the Ethiopian Public Health InsƟtute and Ethiopian Agricultural Research 
InsƟtute, and inputs from the steering commiƩee members. Finally, 992 foods were screened 
for inclusion in the updated EFCT (Figure 1) aŌer considering foods reported in more than one 
source. StarƟng from this iniƟal list of foods, the final decision on the foods that would be 
considered in the updated EFCT was determined by availability of secondary data and possibility 
to generate primary data. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 1: SelecƟon of foods for the EFCT 2025 
 
The naƟonal mandatory food forƟficaƟon for edible oils and wheat flour was not fully in acƟon 
during the study period and thus not considered in the present EFCT.  
The current EFCT includes a total of 727 foods, comprising 582 raw and cooked food entries and 
145 mixed-ingredient dishes. All these foods are grouped into food groups as listed in Table 1.

2. Food groups and codes 
The updated EFCT uses different food groupings and codes compared to previous versions. It 
categorized foods into 17 groups (Table 1) according to their common use. Whenever possible, 
mixed dishes were classified on the basis of their main ingredient. However, in cases where a 
main ingredient could not be idenƟfied, they were placed in the “mixed dishes” group.

Each food entry was assigned a six-digit unique food code, which includes the food group code 
and a unique food number within the food group. The first two digits represent the food group, 
and the last four digits represent the food number in that group. 
2.1 Foods and food descripƟons 
Food names are presented in English and ‘Amharic’ (commonly spoken language in Ethiopia) 
with scienƟfic names where appropriate. The most descripƟve food name was chosen for each 
food, along with relevant descripƟons that influence nutrient content such as edible porƟon, 

6 
 
maturity, color, physical state, cooking method (e.g., raw, boiled) and forƟficaƟon. Unless 
otherwise stated in the descripƟon, composiƟon data refers to the food in its raw form.  
2.1.1 FoodEx2 codes 
FoodEx2 is a comprehensive and flexible food classificaƟon and descripƟon system, created and 
maintained by the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA, 2015) to harmonize the descripƟon 
and  grouping  of foods  across  various  data domains.  Coding  food-related  data  (e.g.  food 
composiƟon, food consumpƟon, food safety, and other food-related data) using FoodEx2 is 
strongly recommended to facilitate naƟonal and internaƟonal data linkages across domains. 
The EFCT 2025 was therefore coded using the FoodEx2 system (Available in the excel datasheet 
10).  
The FoodEx2 code consists of a main base term to describe the food, along with food descriptor 
codes called ‘facets’ that can be added by inserƟng a hash (#) symbol aŌer the base term. Each 
addiƟonal facet is separated by a dollar ($) symbol. The level of detail (added facets) is included 
based on the available informaƟon. In this way, foods can be described in as much detail as 
necessary.  
Example:  for  the  beverage  “tella,  fermented  alcoholic  beverage,  cereal  based  (5.2%  v/v 
alcohol)” the FoodEx2 code is A03MN#F28.A0CQZ$F04.A0ETP$F11.A079Y. This code describes 
tella  with  A03MN  (beer-like  beverages),  process=fermentaƟon  with  F28.A0CQZ, 
ingredient=cereal with F04.A0ETP and alcohol content=5.2% with F11.A079Y. 
Details  about  FoodEx2  can  be  accessed  from: hƩps://www.efsa.europa.eu/en/data/data-
standardisaƟon.  
 
 
 
 
 
 

7 
 
3. Sources of data 
3.1 Primary data 
Primary data were generated for priority foods selected based on specified criteria (details are 
available in secƟon 6). Seventy-two components were considered to generate the data. 
3.2 Secondary data 
Secondary analyƟcal data were collected from the exisƟng EFCTs (part I-IV), 231 published 
scienƟfic arƟcles, 143 unpublished MSc thesis and PhD dissertaƟons, as well as several research 
reports  from  different  insƟtuƟons,  conference  proceedings,  and  laboratory  reports.  The 
collected data were criƟcally reviewed for their suitability for compilaƟon, considering the 
clarity  of  food  descripƟons,  the  methodology  used  for  nutrient  analysis  and  component 
definiƟons to assign appropriate INFOODS component idenƟfiers (also known as tagnames), 
conversion factors applied, units, and mode expression. ComposiƟonal data were collected for 
Ethiopian  foods  established using  an  adapted version  the  FAO/INFOODS  compilaƟon  tool 
(FAO/INFOODS, 2011). AŌer screening the original sources, 2,170 food entries and more than 
16,000 data points were deemed eligible to serve as the basis to build the EFCT 2025.  
3.3 Reference data 
A reference dataset was created using selected internaƟonal FCTs/FCDBs to serve for two 
primary purposes. First, to fill some data gaps by borrowing values for nutrients which were 
missing in the data compiled for Ethiopian foods and second, to verify that the values compiled 
from other sources were reasonable. Annex A lists the FCTs/FCDBs included in the reference 
dataset. 
 
 
 
 
 
 

8 
 
4. Components 
EFCT 2025 includes a total of 69 components which are presented in the Excel Datasheets. Due 
to space and layout consideraƟons, the Condensed Food ComposiƟon Table in the User Guide 
includes only 39 components. The component values are presented per 100 g edible porƟon on 
fresh weight basis (EP). 
The selecƟon of components was guided by the need for informaƟon for major public health 
issues, the current state of nutriƟonal science, the availability and quality of exisƟng data, the 
presence  of  adequate  analyƟcal  methods,  the  feasibility  of  analyƟcal  work,  and  naƟonal 
labeling regulaƟons. To have the users’ perspecƟve on the priority nutrients to be included in 
the EFCT 2025, surveys among potenƟal FCT users in Ethiopia (expert judgment) was also 
conducted. Proximate composiƟon, minerals, vitamins, amino acids (for high protein containing 
foods) and faƩy acids (for high fat containing foods) were the priority nutrients suggested by the 
FCT users.  
Table 2 presents the full list of components, along with units, common analyƟcal methods, 
definiƟons and INFOODS component idenƟfiers (tagnames). Values presented between square 
brackets ([ ]) are of lower quality, either because of a different analyƟcal method, definiƟon or 
expression.

5. SelecƟon of priority foods and sampling design for food sample 
collecƟon 
Among the foods idenƟfied for inclusion in the updated EFCT, priority foods were selected to 
generate primary data (i.e. new data analyzed specifically for the EFCT). The selecƟon criteria 
included high reporƟng frequency in the 2011 Ethiopian NaƟonal Food ConsumpƟon Survey 
(NFCS, 2013) and the Ethiopian Household Income, ConsumpƟon, and Expenditure Surveys 
(HICE 2011; HICE 2016). AddiƟonally, emphasis was placed on popular indigenous foods with 
limited reference data. The priority list was reviewed by the steering commiƩee members for 
final approval. Following this, a naƟonal level sampling was conducted to generate reliable 
nutrient data for the idenƟfied priority foods. Possible food sampling designs were invesƟgated 
through literature reviews and subsequently presented to the steering commiƩee for feedback 
and validaƟon.  
 
Considering the agro-ecological impact on nutrient composiƟon, samples were collected from 
various agro-ecological zones, recognizing the wide diversity in soil and climate condiƟons 
across the country. To implement this, the country was divided into three agro-ecological zones 
based on the alƟtude above sea level of woredas (sampling sites): highland (>2,300 meters), 
midland (1,500–2,300 meters), and lowland (<1,500 meters) (Figure 2).  
 

15 
 
 
Figure 2. Map presenƟng agro-ecological zones (strata) of the woredas in Ethiopia, EFCT 2025 
 
A list of 1039 woredas was idenƟfied based on up-to-date spaƟal resoluƟon data during data 
collecƟon: 413 woredas in highland, 359 woredas in midland, and 267 woredas in lowland 
(Table 3). The sample size was computed using a finite populaƟon formula assuming 90% 
confidence level, 5% margin of error, and 5% sampling sites from the previous Ethiopian FCTs 
parts (III and IV) where 5% of possible sampling sites were covered as aggregate in the FCTs. 
Accordingly, the calculated sample size was 50 sampling sites (woredas) to collect the idenƟfied 
food samples. The sampling sites were then proporƟonally allocated to the woredas in the three 
agro-ecological zones: highland (20), midland (17), and lowland (13). The allocated samples of 
woredas were then randomly selected from the respecƟve agro-ecological zones (Annex C). 
Sample collecƟon in two woredas of Tigray could not take place due to security reasons during 
data collecƟon. Hence, the samples were collected from the 48 sampling woredas. 
 
 
 
 

16

Not available; NS: Not selected.  
5.1 Sampling of priority foods  
Once the sampling woredas were idenƟfied, the samplers explored wholesale markets, large 
retail shops, and other marketplaces within the sampling sites where most of the community 
purchases its food. From the available markets within each sampling site, a total of three 
wholesalers or large retail shops were randomly selected for procuring food items. If the 
number of markets in a sampling woreda was fewer than required (i.e., three), all the three 
samples were collected from the exisƟng market at the sampling site. AddiƟonally, if a specific 
food item was unavailable at the primary sampling shop or market, the samplers moved on to 
the  next  shop  or  market  to  obtain  the  missing  items.  Samples  of  primary  agricultural 
commodiƟes and foods from households were collected between June and August 2022. The 
collecƟon  and  processing  of industrial  products,  fruits,  and  vegetables  took  place  from 
November 2022 to January 2023. The list of samples collected for generaƟng primary analyƟcal 
data is presented in Annex D 
To summarize, the food sampling was conducted in the following three stages:  
Stage 1 (composite at woreda level): equal porƟons of samples collected from three markets of 
the woreda were composited designated as woreda representaƟve samples. 
Stage 2 (composite at regional level): equal porƟons of the woreda-representaƟve samples in 
each region were composited and designated as regional representaƟve samples. 

17 
 
Stage 3 (composite at naƟonal level): equal porƟons of the regional-representaƟve samples 
were pooled together, and a naƟonal composite sample was prepared. 
5.1.1 Sampling of baked foods 
Baked foods, such as enjera and bread, as well as spiced buƩer that were unavailable in the 
market, were collected from ten randomly selected households at the sampling woredas. 
Region-specific tradiƟonal and indigenous foods, including enset, anchote, and camel meat, 
were collected from the areas where the samples were accessible. accessed. 
5.1.2 Sampling of perishable items 
Due to the perishability of samples and logisƟcal challenges in transporƟng them from various 
regions to the EPHI laboratory for preparaƟon, vegetables, fruits, meat (beef, sheep, goat), milk 
and dairy products, poultry, and eggs were sourced from large markets, supermarkets, and 
butcheries in Addis Ababa and its surrounding areas. Although Addis Ababa is not a major 
producer of vegetables and fruits, these commodiƟes are supplied to the city from the country's 
key producƟon regions. 
5.1.3 Sampling of industrial food products 
Industrially manufactured products, such as breakfast cereal, pasta, coffee powder, yoghurt and 
pasteurized milk, were collected from different supermarkets in Addis Ababa. The selecƟon 
process involved invesƟgaƟng the available brands in the country, with emphasis given to the 
major brands. A composite sample was prepared from different brands of products. 
5.1.4 Sampling of fish  
Fish samples were collected from fishing harbors or landing sites in major fish-producing areas: 
Lake Tana, Ziway, Hawassa, Chamo, and the Baro River. These areas are known for producing 
specific fish species. Three fish landing sites were selected from each sampling area, where the 
samples were cleaned, scaled, skinned, eviscerated, deboned, and the edible porƟons were cut 
into small pieces and packed in polyethylene zip-lock bags. The weights of both edible and 
inedible porƟons were recorded. Composite samples were prepared for each sampling site at 
the landing area and transported to the EPHI laboratory at -20°C. AddiƟonally, a further naƟonal 
composite  sample  was  created  by  combining equal  porƟons  of samples  from  different 
producƟon areas. 
5.2 Sample preparaƟon and analysis 
The sample preparaƟon began with the separaƟon of edible and inedible parts of foods 
requiring size reducƟon. The number of samples collected, porƟon sizes for composite sample 

18 
 
preparaƟon, and the edible and inedible porƟons were all recorded. Adequate porƟons from 
the  naƟonally  representaƟve  samples  were  further  processed  as  required  (e.g.,  milled, 
macerated, and dried using oven drying or spray drying). A working sample was then submiƩed 
to the laboratories for composiƟonal analysis. A list showing all analyzed components and the 
respecƟve methodologies used are available on Table 2.  
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 

19 
 
6. Principles of CompilaƟon and CalculaƟon  
6.1 Food data aggregaƟon 
Most raw food entries in EFCT 2025 include data from mulƟple sources and analyses. Data from 
the different sources were matched and aggregated (i.e., an average value was calculated), 
considering  the  food  descripƟon  and  scienƟfic  name  (checking  for  synonyms)  where 
appropriate. Separate food entries were created for foods where notable differences in nutrient 
composiƟon were observed due to color, processing methods, or other factors influencing 
nutrient content, provided sufficient data were available. 
No weighƟng factors were applied to account for variaƟons in producƟon or market share, nor 
were they applied to specific data sources, such as internaƟonal FCTs/FCDBs, in comparison to 
analyƟcal data from scienƟfic arƟcles or analyƟcal reports. 
6.1.1 Assumed values 
Based on current evidence, the values for certain components were assumed to be zero, as 
outlined in Table 4. 
Table 4. Values assumed as zero according to the component and type of food 
Component Assumptions 
Fatty acids and cholesterol assumed zero in foods where total fat value is zero 
Cholesterol assumed as zero in plant-origin foods 
Carbohydrate assumed zero in most fats and oils, in mammal and poultry 
muscle meat, and in fish  
Dietary fiber assumed zero in unprocessed animal-origin foods 
Alcohol assumed as zero for all foods except alcoholic beverages 
Retinol assumed as zero in unfortified plant-origin foods 
Vitamin D assumed as zero in unfortified plant-origin foods 
Vitamin B12 assumed as zero for non-animal and not fortified, except for 
fermented products 
Folic acid assumed as zero in all unfortified foods 
Vitamin C assumed zero in eggs 
Phytate assumed zero in animal-origin foods 
Amino acids assumed zero in foods where total protein value is zero 
 
6.1.2 Borrowing data 
When  analyƟcal  data from  primary  or  secondary  sources  were  unavailable  or  appeared 
unplausible, values were borrowed from reference data. Preference was given to analyƟcal data 

20 
 
from Africa due to geographical similarity, which could influence the nutrient composiƟon of 
foods.  In a few instances, data were borrowed from other similar foods within the EFCT 2025; 
e.g. between raw and dried versions of the same food, by considering appropriate adjustments. 
When borrowing data, the following rules and consideraƟons were taken into account: 
 Proximate composiƟon,  minerals  and  water-soluble  components  were  adjusted  for 
moisture  content  if  the  moisture  content  is  differed  by  more  than  10  percent 
(FAO/INFOODS, 2012); 
 Fat-soluble components (vitamins A, D and E, including carotenoids and reƟnol) were 
not adjusted (Vincent et al, 2020); 
 Faty acids were adjusted for total faƩy acids content (based on faƩy acids expressed as 
percentage); 
 Cholesterol was adjusted based on total fat content when the total fat differed by more 
than 10%. 
 Amino acids were adjusted for total nitrogen content; and 
 All components were adjusted for moisture content when data were borrowed from 
fresh foods for dried versions. 
6.1.3 ConsideraƟons during data aggregaƟon 
i. For data sourced from the Indian Food ComposiƟon Table, values reported as below limit 
of detecƟon (<LOD) in the original FCT were treated as zero. 
ii. For primary analyƟcal data, results were compared to reference data and the following 
assumpƟons were considered to report below limit of quanƟficaƟon (<LOQ) and <LOD: 
 
 If the LOQ value was high, the reported value was recorded as half of the LOQ and 
documented as an esƟmated value ("e"). If the LOQ value was near zero, it was 
recorded as trace ("tr"). 
 If the LOD was very low or closer to zero, it was reported as zero. However, if from the 
reference data the component is known to present in small amounts, it was reported as 
trace (tr).  
 If the LOD was high, a value below the LOD was sourced from reference data. If no 
similar food was available for reference, the value was reported as <LOD. 
 For values compiled from other FCTs, if tr was indicated it was kept as such.  
 
6.1.4 CalculaƟon of cooked foods 
Nutrient values for cooked foods (single-ingredient recipes) were calculated from raw foods, 
considering yield factors and nutrient retenƟon factors. The yield factor esƟmates the changes 
in moisture or fat during cooking while the retenƟon factor esƟmates the loss of or damage to 

21 
 
micronutrients during cooking. Cooked food entries were created for foods that are usually 
cooked, including cereals, starchy root tubers, vegetables, legumes, meat and eggs, as well as 
milk  and  fish.  During  calculaƟon, the  most  common  or  dominant  cooking  method  was 
considered depending on the food group.  
The cooking methods applied were as follows: 
 Steamed, and boiled and drained: Values were calculated using retenƟon factors that 
account for the loss of vitamins and minerals in the discarded cooking water. 
 Boiled as part of a recipe: Values were calculated using retenƟon factors that assume 
the cooking liquid is also consumed, for example rice cooked by absorpƟon and foods 
cooked in Shiro stews. In this case, minerals have a retenƟon factor of 1 (i.e. no loss of 
minerals). 
 Grilled: Values were calculated using retenƟon factors that account for the loss of 
vitamins and minerals in the discarded drippings. For example, grilled chicken. 
 Baking or roasƟng: Values were calculated using retenƟon factors that account for the 
loss of vitamins and minerals. For example, baked kita and roasted fenugreek.  
 
6.1.5 Yield Factors 
For the single-ingredient recipes, most of the yield factors were taken from Bognár (2002), 
Bergstrom  (1994) and MaƩhews and  Garrison (1975). However, a  few yield factors were 
measured at the Food Science and NutriƟon Laboratory Kitchen at EPHI. A list of all food group 
yield factors and their source is presented in Annex E. When yield factors were measured, the 
calculaƟon is based on the following formula: 
푌푖푒푙푑 푓푎푐푡표푟

The weight change in food aŌer cooking is primarily due to water loss or gain. However, in faƩy 
foods, weight changes can also result from fat loss. Fat loss factors were applied to determine 
whether the weight reducƟon was solely due to water loss or a combinaƟon of water and fat 
loss. For foods with a fat content exceeding 5%, the weight change was assumed to result from 
both water and fat loss (except for foods boiled as part of a recipe where no fat loss was 
considered). Therefore, fat loss factors (Table 5) were applied to the cooking methods of 
grilling, steaming, and boiling for the food groups of meat, poultry, and their products and fish 
and their products.

  Fat content of food <5% Fat content of food 5-15% Fat content of food >15% 
Fat loss 0% 7% 13 -15%* 
Water loss 100% 93% 85 – 87%* 
FLF 0 0.07 0.13 
*13% fat loss was used for calculations 
Source: Vincent et al., (2020).

formula:

6.1.6 RetenƟon Factors 
RetenƟon factors esƟmate the loss of components (vitamins, minerals, etc.) during cooking and 
allow the calculaƟon of the nutrient content of a cooked dish from its raw ingredients. The 
main source for the retenƟon factors applied to cooked foods was the EuroFIR technical report 
on recipe calculaƟon (Vásquez-Caicedo et al.,2008) (Annex F). Phytate retenƟon factors were 
extrapolated from original research for uPulses1.0 (FAO/IZiNCG, 2018) and for the Kenya Food 
ComposiƟon Tables (FAO and Government of Kenya, 2018). 
Herbs and spices lacking specific retenƟon factors were assigned values according to their 
source,  as  recommended  by  Vásquez-Caicedo  et  al.  (2008).  Seed-type  spices,  such  as 
cardamom, coriander, mustard, and black pepper, were given the retenƟon factor for pulses; 
leafy herbs and spices, such as oregano and rosemary, were given the retenƟon factor for leafy 
vegetables; and root tuber spices, like turmeric, were assigned the retenƟon factor for root, 
tuber, and bulb categories. However, for water, sugar, salt, yeast, baking powder/soda, and 
ingredients mixed in their raw forms, a retenƟon factor of 1.00 was applied to all nutrients. 
AddiƟonally, not all food groups had a designated retenƟon factor for boiling with cooking 
liquid. When this was unavailable, the retenƟon factor for the stewed version was used. 
 
 
 

23 
 
Generally, the nutrient values in cooked foods are calculated according to  the following 
formula: 
푁푉 푖푛 푐표표푘푒푑 푓표표푑

푌퐹
 
Where, 푁푉 = nutrient value; 퐸푃 = edible porƟon; 푥 is the unit of the component (e.g., g, mg, 
mcg), 푅퐹 = retenƟon factor and 푌퐹 = yield factor 
6.2 Mixed Dishes 
All the mixed dishes presented in the EFCT, which represent major recipes commonly consumed 
in Ethiopia, were prepared at the Food Science and NutriƟon Laboratory Kitchen by professional 
chefs. Data for each mixed dish was collected during preparaƟon, and yield factors were 
calculated as shown in the formula in SecƟon 5.2.1. 
Data collected for mixed dishes comprised:   
 Household measurements of each raw ingredient  
 Total weight in grams of each raw ingredient 
 Edible porƟon weight in grams of each raw ingredient 
 Weight of the final mixed dish aŌer cooking 
 Weight of any inedible porƟons of the final mixed dish aŌer cooking 
 PreparaƟon methods and cooking Ɵme. 
Data collecƟon for mixed dishes included all ingredients, such as water, seasonings, and salt. 
When foods were cooked with an inedible porƟon (e.g., chicken stewed with bones), the 
inedible porƟon was separated from the cooked food, weighed, and subtracted from the raw 
weight. The yield factor includes water used in the dish. Water that was drained and discarded 
was considered as an inedible porƟon. For deep-fried dishes, such as samosa, the amount of oil 
absorbed was esƟmated by measuring the oil before and aŌer deep frying, as well as the oil 
remaining on the paper aŌer frying.  
InformaƟon about the ingredient amounts, final yield and preparaƟon methods for about 145 
mixed dishes are documented in a separate book Ɵtled Ethiopian Foods Recipe. Mixed dishes 
are also listed in the Excel Datasheets (09 Mixed Dishes) with all ingredients, weights, yield 
factors, together with a short descripƟon. 
Nutrient content was calculated using the mixed recipe method, which applies retenƟon factors 
at the ingredient level and yield factors at the recipe level (Figure 3). This method is the most 
recommended and widely used method for esƟmaƟng the composiƟon of mixed dishes (FAO, 

24 
 
2013). The retenƟon factors used in the calculaƟon of mixed dishes were the same as for the 
single-ingredient cooked foods. 
 
Figure 3: RetenƟon factor and yield factor consideraƟons in the mixed recipe method. 푁푉 = 
nutrient value, 푅퐹 = retenƟon factor, 푌퐹 = yield factor (Source: FAO (2013)). 
 
The composiƟon of mixed dishes was calculated based on the following assumpƟons: 
 Trace values (tr) were converted to 0 for calculaƟon. 
 When certain foods lacked nutrient values, values were borrowed from similar foods. 
These borrowed values are not published in EFCT 2025. 
 Some ingredients lacking nutrient data which were used in very small quanƟƟes e.g., 
vanilla powder, were excluded from the calculaƟons based on the assumpƟon that their 
omission will not significantly impact the overall nutriƟonal composiƟon of the mixed 
dish. 
 If an ingredient is missing a component value or has a lower quality value (indicated by 
square brackets), the component value for the mixed dish is also presented in square 
brackets ([ ]). 
 If an ingredient was subjected  to two cooking methods (e.g., boiling followed by 
sautéing),  the  retenƟon  factor  corresponding  to  the  method  associated  with  the 
greatest nutrient loss was applied. 
 Fat losses were not accounted for, as the fat is expected to remain in the mixed dish. 
 
 
 
 
 

25 
 
7. Food and value documentaƟon  
DocumentaƟon at food and value level are presented in EFCT 2024 as detailed below. 
DocumentaƟon at food level 
The data sources for each food entry are indicated by bibliographic idenƟfier codes (BiblioID). 
BiblioIDs and their corresponding sources are listed in Annex G and in the Excel Datasheet 11 
Data sources with BiblioID.  
DocumentaƟon at value level 
The component values derived from the average of selected composiƟonal data for a parƟcular 
food are accompanied by the following staƟsƟcs: 
 Number of data points (n); 
 If the number of data points is 2, minimum (min) and maximum (max); 
 If the number of data points is 3 or greater, min, max, standard deviaƟon (SD) and 
median. 
StaƟsƟcs are not presented for calculated values, i.e., for mixed dishes and cooked foods 
calculated using retenƟon and yield factors, or values calculated based on other components, 
such as sum of proximate components, energy, and vitamin A.  
The type of food composiƟon data presented for each value is documented for all foods 
included in the EFCT 2025. This documentaƟon is referred to as value documentaƟon. 
Values taken from sources outside of Africa are indicated with ‘oa’ to highlight where there are 
gaps in composiƟonal data from Africa.  
The food and value documentaƟon symbols are shown in Table 6.

8. Quality consideraƟons 
The values in EFCT 2025 are derived from primary data (currently analysed data) and secondary 
data (data from the Ethiopian FCTs (parts I–IV), published scienƟfic arƟcles, unpublished MSc 
and PhD thesis, laboratory data, technical reports, conference proceedings, and borrowed data 
from internaƟonal FCTs). The primary analyƟcal data generaƟon process uƟlized standardized 
analyƟcal procedures and quality assurance techniques. The enƟre data were criƟcally reviewed 
during compilaƟon using FAO/INFOODS guidelines. The final values were cross-checked and 
compared with internaƟonal FCTs/FCDBs.  
The data presented in the EFCT 2025 should be interpreted and used considering the following 
quality consideraƟon: 
 VariaƟon in food composiƟon: EFCT 2025 presents the average of available and selected 
data for each food. Therefore, it is important to note that the composiƟon of different food 
samples of the same or similar foods can vary considerably. 
 Comparison of similar foods: When comparing similar foods included in the database (e.g. 
“Barley, pearled, dry, raw” and “Barley, whole grain, hulled, dry, raw”), care should be taken 
since the data usually come  from separate sampling schemes. Differences in  nutrient 
contents may reflect variaƟons in the analyzed samples, rather than expressions of the 
effect of processing. 
 Nutrient contents for mixed dishes: Nutrient contents calculated for mixed dishes are only 
valid as they published in the EFCT 2025. Thus, these nutrient contents may not be accurate 
for mixed dishes with different ingredients and/or ingredient proporƟons. To help users 
assess whether their recipes align with the EFCT 2025, a list of all ingredients, including raw 
weight, cooked weight and short recipe descripƟon, is available for all mixed dishes in the 
Ethiopian Foods Recipe Book 2025 and Excel Datasheet 09. 
 Borrowed data: Most of the values presented for manganese, phytate and vitamin B12 were 
sourced from reference data, with careful aƩenƟon given to food matching considering food 
descripƟons. For folate, apart from the primary data generated for priority foods, the 
majority of values were also sourced from reference data. 
 
 
 

28 
 
9. RecommendaƟons 
Drawing from our experiences and the challenges encountered during the development of the 
EFCT 2025, we recommend the following for future work:   
 Due to limited capacity and shortage of supplies within Ethiopia, majority of the primary 
data generated within this update was conducted in laboratories abroad. Thus, enhancing 
laboratory  capacity  in  terms  of  equipment  and  manpower,  along  with  ensuring  the 
availability of consumables, could support similar future work. 
 A significant porƟon of the food composiƟon data from various sources reviewed during the 
preparaƟon of EFCT 2025 was unusable due to several reasons. To enhance data usability, 
authors publishing food composiƟon data should ensure comprehensive and precise food 
descripƟons, clearly document analyƟcal methods, and specify the basis on which values are 
reported (e.g., whether values were reported on fresh weight or dry maƩer basis). 
 Researchers and potenƟal donors are encouraged to consider esƟmated values and values 
that come from outside Africa to idenƟfy prioriƟes for future food composiƟon analysis. 
 Although  the  EFCT  2025  includes  a  diverse  range  of  foods  and  components,  some 
indigenous foods could not be incorporated due to logisƟcal and financial constraints. We 
recommend that future updates prioriƟze neglected and indigenous foods.  
 ConsideraƟon of mandatory forƟficaƟon to update the exisƟng date shall be done as early 
as possible.  
 We were unable to conduct phytate analysis. However, it is worth analyzing phytate in the 
future, as plant-based foods represent an important part of people’s diet in Ethiopia.     
 The  capacity of  individuals  and  organizaƟons in  Ethiopia need  to be  strengthened to 
produce, use, and disseminate food composiƟon data to ensure a conƟnuous update with 
new analyƟcal data.    
 ConƟnued collaboraƟon among all stakeholders is crucial to strengthening and sustaining 
food composiƟon data producƟon efforts. The  ulƟmate  goal should be to establish  a 
comprehensive  food  composiƟon  data  plaƞorm  supported  by  a  reliable  food  analysis 
laboratory  and  an  accessible,  regularly  updated  and  user-friendly  food  composiƟon 
database. 
 
 
 

29
